Research shows that papers with ethnically diverse authors receive more
citations and have higher impact factors than those with homogeneous
authorship.

Additionally, [**diverse** scientific communities are more **likely** to
**identify** research questions **relevant** to **underserved**
populations]{.underline} and address blind spots in experimental design.
For example, medical research conducted by homogeneous teams has
historically overlooked sex-based differences in drug metabolism,
leading to adverse outcomes that might have been anticipated with more
diverse research teams.

#### Scientists [quit]{.underline} and [no one]{.underline} replaces them. That [destroys]{.underline} the scientific economy.

**Paquette '25** \[Andre; May; Co-host of The Deep Dive with Andre, an
independent research podcast that investigates complex global issues,
drawing from a wide array of credible academic, journalistic, and
governmental sources, podcast has earned a reputation for clarity,
intellectual rigor, and accessible longform breakdowns of high-stakes
global trends; The Deep Dive with Andre, "The Precarious State of
American Science: Projecting a Potential Exodus of U.S. Scientists,"
the-precarious-state-of\--02ebtwv.gamma.site\] //jwh

Even before scientists consider leaving the country, the
administration\'s actions appear to be fostering an internal \"**[brain
drain]{.underline}**\" [within the U.S. scientific system]{.underline}.
This phenomenon [manifests]{.underline} as scientists self-censoring
their research topics, shifting their focus away from controversial (yet
potentially critical) areas, or becoming hesitant to pursue careers in
government service. This subtle exodus of talent and ideas **[represents
an immediate threat to scientific innovation and policy
development]{.underline}**.

This [**internal** shift represents a **loss** of scientific
**dynamism**]{.underline} and direction that precedes, and [can
**exacerbate**]{.underline}, [any subsequent **external**
exodus]{.underline}. Reports indicate that scientists are actively
removing \"politically-charged keywords\" such as \"climate change\" or
\"DEI\" from their grant proposals to enhance their chances of funding.
This trend is particularly pronounced among early-career researchers who
feel most vulnerable to funding uncertainties and political shifts.

The [**consequences** of this **internal** brain drain are
**far-reaching**]{.underline}, [affecting]{.underline} not only current
research outputs but also long-term scientific capacity building and
institutional knowledge retention. When scientists avoid certain
research domains due to political pressures rather than scientific
merit, **[the entire knowledge ecosystem suffers]{.underline}** from
artificial constraints on inquiry.

Graduate students are similarly adapting, focusing less on explicit
terms related to diversity, equity, and inclusion or climate change, and
instead emphasizing broader, more neutral terms like \"community-based
work\" or \"global change\". Faculty advisors report guiding students
toward \"safer\" research questions that are less likely to attract
political scrutiny or funding challenges, potentially steering promising
young scientists away from critically important research areas.

The fear of being targeted for pursuing research in certain areas, such
as climate science (which faces defunding and political criticism) or
DEI-related work (which is under scrutiny in university accreditation
and funding), can lead to widespread self-censorship or a reluctance
among researchers to enter or continue in these fields. Survey data
suggests that up to 30% of scientists in politically sensitive fields
have modified their research questions or methodologies to avoid
potential controversy.

Particularly concerning is the long-term impact on scientific leadership
positions. [As **senior** scientists in **government**
agencies]{.underline} retire or **[leave]{.underline}** their positions,
[**qualified** replacements]{.underline} may [be **deterred** from
**stepping** into these roles]{.underline} due to concerns about
political interference or job security, [creating **leadership** gaps in
**critical** scientific institutions]{.underline} that inform policy
decisions across multiple sectors.

### Impact\-\--Pandemics\-\--2NC

#### Pandemics cause [extinction]{.underline} absent a [functioning]{.underline} scientific system.

**TEF '25** \[The Elders Foundation; January; Founded by Nelson Mandela,
composed of former heads of state, UN officials, and Nobel Peace Prize
laureates, includes figures like Ban Ki-moon (former UN
Secretary-General) and Mary Robinson (former UN High Commissioner for

theelders.org/sites/default/files/2025-01/The%20Elders%20Pandemics%20Paper%202025%20-%20English.pdf\]
//jwh

[The **growing** risk of pandemics confronts leaders with an
**existential challenge**]{.underline}. **[The science is
clear]{.underline}**. [The next pandemic threat is a **matter of when**,
not if]{.underline}. [There is a **significant** chance that a
**future** pandemic could be some combination of **more
infectious**]{.underline}, [**more** likely to **mutate** and **more
fatal** than COVID-19]{.underline}.

[The **world cannot afford to wait to prepare**]{.underline} for the
next pandemic. Environmental degradation, [**large-scale movement** of
people and an ever **hotter** planet are **increasing** the **risk** of
**transmission** of pathogens from **animals** to
**humans**]{.underline}, and the emergence of more environments that are
hospitable for disease-carrying species.

[The **next pandemic** may not be naturally occurring]{.underline}.
[Expertise in **engineering deadly pathogens** is
expanding]{.underline}. [There is **insufficient global
oversight**]{.underline} of biosafety and biosecurity risks. But despite
all these risks, [**leaders** are **failing to act**]{.underline}. [The
**cycle** of **panic and neglect**]{.underline} that the world has seen
following previous Public Health Emergencies of International Concern
declared by the World Health Organization continues. [The lessons
**identified** from **previous pandemics** are not being
applied]{.underline}. Most of the recommendations from numerous
independent expert panels have not been implemented.

Many leaders continue to see [pandemics as a **health**
problem]{.underline}. Yet it is clear that the [**impacts** of pandemics
are **economy-wide** and **society-wide**]{.underline}.
[**Misinformation** and **disinformation** about vaccines]{.underline}
and the role of the World Health Organization with respect to global
public health emergencies [persist]{.underline}. There has not yet been
the foresight to invest sufficiently in pandemic prevention,
preparedness and response to reduce the risks of another disastrous
global pandemic, even as the global economy takes years to recover from
the trillions lost from COVID-19. The significant social and health
impacts are ongoing.

At The Elders, we embarked on our pandemic strategy in the wake of the
COVID-19 response. We watched with dismay how nationalism, failure of
leadership and the unchecked power of the pharmaceutical industry led to
vast disparities in access to vaccines, diagnostics and treatments. We
learned of a [**fragmented** and **dysfunctional** global surveillance
system]{.underline}, which punished rather than rewarded countries
[which shared vital biological and genetic data]{.underline}. We saw the
disproportionate impact of the pandemic on women, caregivers and many
other vulnerable communities. In response, we joined with civil society
and other global leaders in calling for a clear set of global policies,
agreed upon at the highest levels of leadership, which ensure equity and
rights in pandemic prevention, preparedness and response.

[COVID-19 was the **worst pandemic** in **modern** times]{.underline}.
It [caused]{.underline} an estimated **[28 million excess
deaths]{.underline}**, [and **tens of trillions** of dollars in economic
losses. There was **further immeasurable impact** on the **wider human
fabric** of our societies]{.underline}, [including **unknown longer-term
damage**]{.underline} (in particular on children and adolescents). But
[a future pandemic could be much worse]{.underline}. **[Pandemics are
inequitable]{.underline}**. **[They hit the most vulnerable
people]{.underline}** and the countries least prepared for global shocks
hardest. During COVID-19 we witnessed large-scale injustice in how
poorer countries were unable to access the vaccines, diagnostics and
treatments they so desperately needed.

This is a collective action problem. We learned from COVID-19 that
**[none of us are safe unless all of us are safe]{.underline}**. Yet,
[**humanity** is **struggling** to put aside **individual** and
**national** interests and act in our **common interest**]{.underline}.
Some people and governments are actively turning against actions that
would significantly reduce risks. How can we break through this impasse?

#### That's [exacerbated]{.underline} sans scientific [support]{.underline}.

**Buchler '25** \[Emily; March 28; Senior writer at Johns Hopkins

\"**[Science is slow]{.underline}**,\" says Gigi Gronvall, sitting at
her desk one morning in front of the images that remind her of [the
**knowledge** and **benefits** a society can **reap** when **scientific
breakthrough**s occur]{.underline}. \"But **[science is far more
effective than jumping to conclusions]{.underline}**.\"

[During the **pandemic**]{.underline}, with [schools]{.underline} and

### Impact\-\--Biodiversity\-\--2NC

#### Biodiversity [loss]{.underline} causes [extinction]{.underline}. 

**Weston '25** \[Phoebe; March 26; Biodiversity correspondent for The
Guardian; The Guardian, "Biodiversity loss in all species and every
ecosystem linked to humans -- report,"
theguardian.com/environment/2025/mar/26/human-link-biodiversity-loss-species-ecosystems-climate-pollution-eawag-study-nature-aoe\]
//jwh

Humans are driving [biodiversity **loss** among **all
species**]{.underline} across the planet, according to a synthesis of
more than 2,000 studies.

The exhaustive global analysis [leaves **no doubt** about the
**devastating impact**]{.underline} humans are having on Earth,
according to researchers from the Swiss Federal Institute of Aquatic
Science and Technology (Eawag) and the University of Zurich. The
[study]{.underline} -- which [accounted for]{.underline} nearly
[**100,000 sites** across all continents]{.underline} --
[found]{.underline} that human activities had resulted in
"**[unprecedented effects on biodiversity]{.underline}**", according to
the paper, published in Nature.

Florian Altermatt, professor of aquatic ecology at the University of
Zurich and head of Eawag, said: "[It is **one** of the
**largest**]{.underline} syntheses of the human
**[impacts]{.underline}** on biodiversity ever conducted
**[worldwide]{.underline}**."

The team looked at terrestrial, freshwater and marine habitats, as well
as including all groups of organisms, including microbes, fungi, plants,
invertebrates, fish, birds and mammals.

Human pressures distinctly shifted community composition (essentially,
which species live where) and decreased local diversity, researchers
found. On average, the number of species at human-impacted sites was
almost 20% lower than at sites unaffected by humans.

Particularly [**severe** losses]{.underline} were [recorded among
**reptiles**]{.underline}, [**amphibians** and **mammals**]{.underline},
according to the paper. Their populations are often smaller than
invertebrates, which **[increases]{.underline}** the [chances of
**extinction**]{.underline}.

The analysis covered [**five** drivers of decline]{.underline}:
**[habitat change]{.underline}**, direct [**exploitation** of
resources]{.underline} (such as hunting or fishing), [climate
**change**]{.underline}, [**invasive** species and
**pollution**]{.underline}.

Franois Keck, lead author and a postdoctoral researcher in Altermatt's
research group, said: "Our findings [show]{.underline} that
[all]{.underline} five factors [have a **strong impact** on
biodiversity]{.underline} worldwide, in all groups of organisms and in
all ecosystems."

Pollution and habitat changes, often driven by agriculture, have a
particularly negative impact on biodiversity. Intensive agriculture --
especially arable farming -- involves large amounts of pesticides and
fertilisers, which result in a decline of biodiversity, but also shifts
the composition of species. The full extent of climate change and how it
affects species is not entirely understood.

While [the overall effect]{.underline} of human intervention [was
negative]{.underline}, some ambiguities remained: human impact on
wildlife varied by location, as did the degree to which biodiversity was
homogenised by human activity, researchers said.

Before this paper there had never been an attempt to combine findings
from such a large number of biodiversity research studies examining
humans' impact everywhere on the planet and in all groups of organisms;
most studies were limited to looking at either a single location or a
specific human impact. This meant it was difficult to make general
statements about the impacts of human activity on biodiversity,
researchers say.

Keck said: "**[It's not just the number of species that is
declining]{.underline}**. Human **[pressure is also changing the
composition of species communities]{.underline}**."

In mountainous areas, for example, specialised plants are being replaced
by those that typically grow at lower altitudes. This process is known
as the "elevator to extinction" as high-altitude plants have nowhere
else to go. This could mean that while the number of species might
remain the same, the diversity is reduced.

"[**Bending** the **curve** of **contemporary** biodiversity
loss]{.underline} and change [is **one** of the **greatest** challenges
facing our society]{.underline}," the researchers stated. They said the
paper should provide an "important benchmark" for the development and
assessment of future conservation strategies.

funding]{.underline}. The universities argued the move threatened

and other technology fields.

capricious and contrary to law.

At issue are "indirect" costs, expenses such as building maintenance and
computer systems that aren't linked directly to a specific project.
Currently, the NSF determines each grant recipient's indirect costs
individually and is supposed to cover actual expenses.

The Trump administration has dismissed indirect expenses as "overhead"
and capped them for future awards by the NSF to universities at 15 % of
the funding for direct research costs.

The University of California, one of the plaintiffs, estimated the
change would cost it just under \$100 million a year.

[Judges have blocked **similar** caps]{.underline} that [the **Trump
administration** placed on grants]{.underline} by the Energy Department
and the National Institutes of Health.

### Link\-\--Science Diplomacy\-\--1NC

#### International cooperation takes scientists away from the NSF.

**McLaughlin '21** \[Jacqueline, Gad Perry, Kevin Manuel, Kelly Soluri,
and Andrew Hebbeler; September 23; Associate Professor of Biology at
Penn State University-Lehigh Valley, former Visiting Professor at
Jiangnan University, former Jefferson Science Fellow at the US
Department of State and Office of S&T Cooperation, former Embassy
Science Fellow at the US Embassy in Bucharest, Founding Director of Penn
State's CHANCE program, and Special Counselor to the Vice-Rector of
Research at the University of Bucharest; American Association for the
Advancement of Science, "The U.S. Embassy Science Fellows Program:
Implementation and Impacts,"
sciencediplomacy.org/article/2021/us-embassy-science-fellows-program-implementation-and-impacts\]
//jwh

[[**International** cooperation in science]{.mark}]{.underline} and
technology (S&T) plays an important role in driving scientific
discovery, enhancing economic prosperity, and addressing broader foreign
policy interests. One such program that infuses S&T into foreign policy
is the U.S. Department of State's (DoS) Embassy Science Fellows (ESF)
program, which is designed to [**[promote]{.mark}** scientific
[personnel deployment]{.mark} in support of **diplomacy**]{.underline}.
The aim of this paper is to utilize nearly two decades of data to assess
the ESF program from a U.S. DoS perspective. We analyzed Fellows'
perceptions of their ESF experience, obtained through both quantitative
and qualitative surveys, to identify both successful program elements
and areas that might benefit from further improvements. Since 2001,
[the]{.underline} U.S. [[government]{.underline}]{.mark} has
[[dispatched]{.underline}]{.mark} approximately [[**500**
science]{.underline}]{.mark} and technology
[[experts]{.underline}]{.mark}, spanning twenty-five federal agencies,
[[most often from the]{.underline}]{.mark} U.S. Environmental Protection
Agency, **[[National Science Foundation]{.underline}]{.mark}**, and U.S.
Department of Agriculture (accounting for over 50% of ESF). Through
2001-2019, requests for ESFs Fellows were submitted by U.S. Embassies
and Consulates [in **142** countries]{.underline}. In response, the
program was able to [[send experts to **116**
countries]{.underline}]{.mark}, with deployments in China (N=51),
Portugal (N=15), and the Philippines (N=14) leading. We found it
encouraging that most Fellows reported favorable assessments of the
program and its results and benefits to Fellows and hosts. They also
reported that there was expanded international S&T cooperation and
enhanced understanding of U.S. foreign policy goals. Also encouraging
was the finding that home agencies benefitted following their employees'
ESF experience, as Fellows were better poised to advance the diverse,
agency mission and priorities. The main sources of ESF dissatisfaction
identified by Fellows were perceived isolation and insufficiently clear
aims. Overall, this paper finds that the ESF program has been successful
in terms of providing benefits to hosts, Fellows, and their employing
agencies, and in expanding international S&T cooperation. We offer our
perspective on opportunities for improvement and areas for further study
and propose the ESF program as a model for other countries.

BOSTON (AP) --- A [federal [judge]{.mark} has [blocked]{.mark} President
Donald [Trump]{.mark} ['s]{.mark} administration from making drastic
[cuts]{.mark} [to]{.mark} research funding provided by the [National
Science Foundation]{.mark}.]{.underline}

U.S. District Judge Indira Talwani in Boston [struck down on Friday a
policy change that could have stripped universities of tens of millions
of dollars in research funding. The universities argued the move
threatened critical work in artificial intelligence, cybersecurity,
semiconductors and other technology fields]{.underline}.

Talwani said the [change]{.mark}, announced by the NSF in May, was
[arbitrary and capricious and [contrary to law.]{.mark}]{.underline}

An email Saturday to the NSF was not immediately returned.

At issue are "indirect" costs, expenses such as building maintenance and
computer systems that aren't linked directly to a specific project.
Currently, the [NSF [determines each grant
recipient']{.mark}]{.underline}[s]{.mark} indirect costs individually
and is supposed to cover actual expenses.

## Internal

### Internal\-\--Scientific Research\-\--2NC

#### The NSF is the [lynchpin]{.underline} of [modern]{.underline} scientific [research]{.underline}.

**Lane '25** \[Neal and Michael Riordan; June 18; Physicist and former
director of the National Science Foundation and science adviser to
President Bill Clinton; The Washington Post, "Trump is undermining U.S.
science. Here's why that's dangerous,"
washingtonpost.com/opinions/2025/06/18/trump-science-attacks-internet\]
//jwh

Established in 1950 at the outset of the Cold War, [the NSF]{.underline}
was the realization of a concept envisioned by Vannevar Bush in his
famous 1945 report, "Science: The Endless Frontier."
[Focus]{.underline}ed [on]{.underline} basic [**research**
in]{.underline} the physical [sciences]{.underline}, the agency grew
slowly but steadily during the 1950s, experiencing a burst of political
support and funding growth after the launch of the Soviet Union's
Sputnik I satellite shocked the nation in 1957.

At its heart was a management ethos borrowed from successful wartime
research: the idea that [**knowledgeable** scientists could identify the
**most fruitful** topics and the best ways to **pursue**
them]{.underline}. The NSF usually recruits experienced scientists to
manage its programs, [**bringing** them from **universities** and
research institutes]{.underline} to its Virginia headquarters for a few
years' duration. [**Thousands** of]{.underline} other [scientists have
served on its **peer-review** panels]{.underline}.

The [agency play]{.underline}ed a [**leading role** in bringing the
internet]{.underline} and World Wide Web to a broad spectrum of users.
Its NSFNET computer network, originally intended for the academic and
educational pursuits of scientists at U.S. universities, was opened to
public and commercial use in 1991, serving [as the
internet's]{.underline} "[backbone]{.underline}." The first widely
available Web browser --- Mosaic, the prototype of many subsequent
browsers --- was developed by two programmers at the NSF-funded National
Center for Supercomputing Applications at the University of Illinois.
And during the mid-1990s, NSF supported Stanford University's Digital
Library Initiative, in which graduate students Larry Page and Sergey
Brin developed the guts of their Google search engine.

Another [**outstanding** NSF **contribution** is the]{.underline} Laser
Interferometer Gravitational- Wave Observatory (**[LIGO]{.underline}**),
[which]{.underline} has [recorded **incredibly faint** space-time
**ripples** triggered **eons** earlier by **cataclysmal black hole
mergers**]{.underline}. Its program managers' risk-taking and patience
were rewarded by this breakthrough, for which three LIGO scientists
received the 2017 Nobel Prize in physics. The NSF's farsighted
management of this project, [sustained over **three
decades**]{.underline}, has also [stimulated advances in laser and
quantum tech]{.underline}nologies.

[The **foundation**]{.underline} has also [supported **cutting-edge
research** in microbiology]{.underline} --- for example, [that of
Jennifer Doudna]{.underline}, who shared the [**2020 Nobel Prize**
for]{.underline} the discovery of the [**gene-editing**
tech]{.underline}nology [that is now the **primary** tool for
**research** in molecular biology]{.underline}.

These striking examples underscore the [**benefits** and **wisdom** of
allowing **NSF** scientist]{.underline}-managers sufficient [**freedom**
to support **long-term** basic-**research** projects]{.underline} whose
outcomes cannot be anticipated. That was the management ethos followed
by Bush and his World War II-era Office of Scientific Research and
Development, which led to many decisive weapons technologies.

#### The NSF is [key]{.underline} to [worldwide]{.underline} scientific research and American [prosperity]{.underline}.

**EarthSky '25** \[EarthSky; May 21; Established in 1991, science media
organization that translates complex research into accessible reporting
for the public, received multiple awards for excellence in science
communication, including honors from the American Meteorological
Society, partners with leading scientists to ensure accuracy, editorial
team includes science journalists with decades of experience, syndicated
on over 1,800 radio stations, widely recognized as a trusted source for
astronomy, Earth science, and environmental reporting; EarthSky, "How
cuts to science in the US endanger everyone,"
earthsky.org/human-world/cuts-to-science-in-the-us-nsf\] //jwh

[NSF **investments**]{.underline} have [made **America** and **American
science** great]{.underline}. At least [**268 Nobel laureates** received
**NSF grants**]{.underline} during their careers. [The foundation has
**partnered** with **agencies** across the government]{.underline} since
it was created, [including those dealing with **national security** and
**space exploration**]{.underline}. The Federal Reserve estimates that
[government-supported **research** from the NSF]{.underline} and other
agencies has [had a **return** on **investment** of]{.underline} 150% to
[**300%** since 1950]{.underline}, meaning for every dollar U.S.
taxpayers invested, they got back between \$1.50 and \$3.

However, that funding is now at risk.

Since January, layoffs, leadership resignations and a massive proposed
reorganization have threatened the integrity and mission of the National
Science Foundation. Hundreds of research grants have been terminated.
The administration's proposed federal budget for fiscal year 2026 would
cut NSF's funding by 55%, an unprecedented reduction that would end
federal support for science research across a wide range of discipines.

At my own geology lab, I have seen [NSF grants **catalyze research** and
the work of dozens of students who have collected **data** that's now
**used** to **reduce** risks from **earthquakes**]{.underline},
**[floods]{.underline}**, **[landslides]{.underline}**,
**[erosion]{.underline}**, [**sea**-level **rise** and **melting
glaciers**]{.underline}.

I have also served on advisory committees and review panels for the NSF
over the past 30 years and have seen the value the foundation produces
for the American people.

American science's greatness stemmed from war

[In the 1940s]{.underline}, [with the **advent** of **nuclear
weapons**]{.underline}, [the space race and the **intensification** of
the **Cold War**]{.underline}, American science and engineering
expertise [became **increasingly critical** for national
**defense**]{.underline}. At the time, most basic and applied research
was done by the military.

Vannevar Bush, an electrical engineer who oversaw military research
efforts during World War II, including development of the atomic bomb,
had a different idea.

He articulated an expansive scientific vision for the United States in
Science: The Endless Frontier. The report was a [**blueprint** for an
**American research juggernaut** grounded in the **expertise** of
university **faculty**]{.underline}, staff and graduate students.

On May 10, 1950, after five years of debate and compromise, President
Harry Truman signed legislation creating the National Science
Foundation. It put Bush's vision to work. Since then, [the
**foundation** has become the **leading funder** of basic **research**
in the **United States**]{.underline}.

NSF's mandate, then as now, was to [support **basic research** and
spread funding for science across **all 50 states**]{.underline}.
Expanding America's scientific workforce was and [**remains** integral
to **American prosperity**]{.underline}. By 1952, the foundation was
awarding merit fellowships to graduate and postdoctoral scientists from
every state.

There were compromises. Control of NSF rested with presidential
appointees, disappointing Bush. He wanted scientists in charge to avoid
political interference with the foundation's research agenda.

**[NSF funding matters to everyone]{.underline}**,
**[everywhere]{.underline}**

Today, American tax dollars supporting science go to every state in the
union.

The states with the most NSF grants awarded between 2011 and 2024
include several that voted Republican in the 2024 election -- Texas,
Florida, Michigan, North Carolina and Pennsylvania -- and several that
voted Democratic, including Massachusetts, New York, Virginia and
Colorado.

[More than **1,800 public** and **private** institutions]{.underline},
scattered across all 50 states, [receive NSF funding]{.underline}. [The
grants pay the **salaries** of **staff**]{.underline}, [**faculty** and
**students**]{.underline}, [boosting **local employment** and supporting
college towns and cities]{.underline}. For states with major research
universities, those grants [add up to **hundreds of millions** of
dollars **each year**]{.underline}. Even states with few universities
each see tens of millions of dollars for research.

#### The NSF is [necessary]{.underline} for a [future]{.underline} workforce that supports scientific [innovation]{.underline}.

**NSF '25** \[National Science Foundation; June 24; Independent agency
of the United States federal government that supports fundamental
research and education in all the non-medical fields of science and

[The]{.underline} U.S. **[N]{.underline}**ational
**[S]{.underline}**cience **[F]{.underline}**oundation has
[announced]{.underline} a [**\$25.5 million** investment to **support**
fundamental **research** and]{.underline} workforce [development aimed
at enabling **future generations** of U.S. manufacturing]{.underline}.
This year\'s awards will [support]{.underline} seven [**research
grants** and **nine seed projects** across **36 institutions** and
companies through the **NSF**]{.underline} Future Manufacturing (NSF FM)
[program]{.underline}.

The NSF FM program [focuses on areas such as
**biomanufacturing**]{.underline}, [**cyber manufacturing** and

**intersections** with **quantum** manufacturing]{.underline}. The

**[transformative]{.underline}** manufacturing
**[capabilities]{.underline}**, [going **far beyond** improvements to

\"The FM program targets [**critical technical gaps** and **lays** the

This year\'s FM awards include:

Seven research grants, each receiving up to \$3 million over a four-year
period, to support multidisciplinary teams conducting fundamental
research to enable new manufacturing capabilities, materials, or
systems. Projects span a range of topics, including bioengineering in
resource-constrained environments, \"recyclofacturing\" using artificial
intelligence to create products from metal scrap, and using robotics and
digital twins for additive manufacturing of multi-material systems.

Nine seed grants, each receiving up to \$500,000 over a two-year period,
to support early-stage teams exploring novel concepts and partnerships
that could shape future directions in manufacturing. Seed projects
include efforts in future photonic quantum manufacturing using DNA,
self-learning tools to create superconducting circuits, using
cyanobacteria and water for low-energy cement manufacturing, and
enabling domestic lithium extraction from unconventional sources.

By [**advancing** research in]{.underline} areas [such as the

**technology**]{.underline}, [expands **innovation**]{.underline}
capacity, [and prepares a **skilled** American

#### The NSF [funds]{.underline} the following [fifty two]{.underline} companies that are [key]{.underline} to the [modern]{.underline} scientific [industry]{.underline}.

**ASF '25** \[America\'s Seed Fund; 2025; Invests up to \$2 million in
early-stage startups, supports deep tech and scientific innovation,
operates through rigorous merit review by Ph.D.-level experts, enables

America\'s Seed Fund, "NSF-Funded Companies: In the News,"

Each month, America's Seed Fund powered by the U.S. [National Science
Foundation shares]{.underline} news [**stories** from NSF-funded
**startups**]{.underline}. Find below news highlights from [**select**
companies]{.underline} previously [**funded** by the NSF]{.underline}
Small Business Innovation Research/Small Business Technology Transfer
(SBIR/STTR) **[programs]{.underline}**:

**[Ambi Robotics]{.underline}**

Ambi Robotics (NSF-2111915) designs AI-powered robotic systems that help
sort a wide variety of packages for shipping, e-commerce and logistics.
The company is increasing production to meet growing customer demand.
Article via Robotics & Automation News.

**[Avicena]{.underline}**

Using microLED technology, Avicena (NSF-2151747) develops and
manufactures ultra-low energy optical links for AI supercomputers and
data centers. The company recently raised \$65 million. Article via
Optics.org.

**[Ecolectro]{.underline}**

Ecolectro (NSF-1951215) develops low-cost renewable hydrogen
electrolyzers using membrane technology. The company is partnering to
revitalize American manufacturing and scale low-cost hydrogen. Article
via Fuel Cells Works.

**[Ground Control Robotics]{.underline}**

Centipede-inspired robots developed by Ground Control Robotics
(NSF-2335553) can navigate difficult farm terrains and remove weeds from
crops. Using sensors and AI, the robotic centipedes help farmers collect
data about plant health or soil conditions while reducing labor costs.
Article via IEEE Spectrum.

**[Harvest]{.underline}**

Harvest, formerly known as Harvest Thermal (NSF-2127147), develops a
smart thermal battery system that heats, cools and provides hot water in
homes. The technology stores heat in a water tank and could reduce
energy bills by up to 30%. Article via The Cool Down.

**[Labby]{.underline}**

Labby (NSF-2233881) develops advanced technology for raw milk testing
using an artificial intelligence-enabled optical sensor that uses light
scattering, absorption and fluorescence to test cow milk for diseases
and monitor the health of the cow. Article via Cornell CALS.

**[Raydiant Oximetry]{.underline}**

To improve health outcomes for mothers and babies during childbirth,
Raydiant Oximetry (NSF-2025901) develops a noninvasive sensor technology
that directly measures fetal oxygen levels. The company is partnering
with GE Healthcare to advance fetal monitoring capabilities. Article via
MassDevice.

**[Silvus Technologies]{.underline}**

To support frontline operations in challenging environments, Silvus
Technologies (NSF-0620588) develops software for mobile ad-hoc network
technology that secures data, voice and video communications. The
company was recently acquired by Motorola Solutions for \$4.4 billion.
Article via Bloomberg Law.

**[South 8 Technologies]{.underline}**

South 8 Technologies (NSF-1831087) develops novel lithium-ion batteries
that perform better in extreme temperatures. The company\'s technology
improves battery performance, safety and range. Article via UC San Diego
Today.

**[XL Batteries]{.underline}**

XL Batteries (NSF-2240504) develops a new \"flow\" battery that stores
energy for up to weeks at a time. The technology is gaining attention
from data center operators searching for safe, low-cost and reliable
grid-scale energy storage. Article via CleanTechnica.

**[Cleancult]{.underline}**

Cleancult (NSF-1842921) develops sustainable cleaning products using
non-toxic ingredients. The company recently launched products at 300
Costco locations nationwide. Article via AL Circle

**[Creative BioTherapeutics]{.underline}**

Creative BioTherapeutics (NSF-2150149) develops biologic therapies for
late-stage diseases. The company's technology is a biologic therapy
targeting immune system evasive drug-resistant cancer either alone or in
conjunction with standard of care. Article via Life Sciences Review

**[Electra]{.underline}**

Using electrochemical technology, Electra (NSF-2039232) transforms iron
ore into 99% pure iron for steelmakers and battery manufacturers. The
company just recently raised \$186 million to build a demonstration
plant in Colorado. Article via Inc. Magazine

**[Flora Coatings]{.underline}**

Flora Coatings (NSF-1830986) develops high-performance and sustainable
anti-corrosion coatings that serve industries, including automotive,
aviation and healthcare. The company recently partnered with Capital Q
Ventures. Article via FOX4 News

**[Graymatter Robotics]{.underline}**

Graymatter Robotics (NSF-2126915) develops robots to help workers
complete repetitive and ergonomically challenging tasks with efficiency
and productivity. The company was selected as one of the top 50 most
innovative robotics companies by The Robot Report. Article via The Robot
Report

**[rStream]{.underline}**

Using artificial intelligence and robotics, rStream (NSF-2423645)
automates waste streams in real-time to revamp recycling and compost
collection systems. The company's technology sorts waste into recycling,
compost and trash containers with 90% accuracy. Article via UMass
Amherst

**[Sironix Renewables]{.underline}**

Sironix Renewables (NSF-1853106) turns soybeans and coconuts into
surfactants, an additive for hygiene products used in shampoos and
laundry detergents, replacing petroleum-based products. Article via The
Cool Down

**[SpadXTech]{.underline}**

SpadXTech (NSF-2310226) uses synthetic biology to develop biomaterials
as alternatives to traditional materials like leather, insulation and
plastic films. The company was selected as one of the winners for the
2025 Manufacturing Excellence Awards by the Worcester Business Journal.
Article via Worcester Business Journal

**[Vaxess Technologies]{.underline}**

Vaxess Technologies (NSF-1632434) develops a vaccine and drug delivery
system to transform home delivery of therapies and vaccines. The company
recently raised \$9 million. Article via Drug Delivery Business News

**[AeroShield Materials]{.underline}**, Gallox Semiconductors and
spotLESS Material

AeroShield Materials (NSF-2155248), Gallox Semiconductors (NSF-2451404)
and spotLESS Materials (NSF-2026140) were selected as winners for the
2025 Hello Tomorrow Global Challenge. The 2025 Hello Tomorrow Global
Challenge is an international, early-stage deep tech startup competition
across all industries, including aerospace, energy, food and
agriculture, digital health and medical devices, and more. Article via
Hello Tomorrow

**[Apptronik]{.underline}**

Robotics company Apptronik (NSF-1747193) develops an artificial
intelligence-powered humanoid robot named Apollo. The 5\'8" 160-pound
robot is designed to perform a variety of tasks, including lifting heavy
objects, unloading trailers, and operating tools in factories and
warehouses. Apptronik recently raised \$403 million. Article via IoT
World Today

**[Atom Computing]{.underline}**

Atom Computing (NSF-1951188) was named one of the top 10 most innovative
computing companies of 2025 by Fast Company. Atom Computing develops
quantum computers using pulses of light to manipulate neutral atoms.
Article via Fast Company

**[Coreshell]{.underline}**

To reduce the cost of lithium-ion batteries and improve the range of
electric vehicles, Coreshell (NSF-2052172) develops a silicon anode
coating that goes directly onto the electrodes of a battery. The company
recently raised \$24 million. Article via TechCrunch

**[Cruz Foam]{.underline}**

Cruz Foam (NSF-2054360) is partnering with Tempest, a weather solutions
tech company, to deliver biodegradable foam packaging made from shrimp
shells and food waste. The new packaging will be launched in June this
year. Article via Packaging Gateway

**[Mapless AI]{.underline}**

Mapless AI (NSF-2240322) develops technology that allows for the safe
remote control of vehicles from thousands of miles away. The company is
participating in a pilot program in Detroit that helps residents access
low-cost transportation. Article via Forbes

**[Nitricity]{.underline}**

With a recent pivot to include recycled almond shells, Nitricity
(NSF-2111864) uses air, water and electricity to develop a plant-based,
pathogen-free nitrogen fertilizer to improve field production. The
company recently raised \$10 million. Article via AgFunderNews

**[Noble Gas System]{.underline}**

Noble Gas Systems (NSF-2223187) develops safe, lightweight, and
conformable gas storage and delivery systems for hydrogen and other
compressed gases. The company recently raised \$4.2 million. Article via
Pulse 2.0

**[ReSource Chemical]{.underline}**

ReSource Chemical (NSF-2301417) uses biomass and carbon dioxide to make
high-performing plastics. The company recently raised \$15 million.
Article via Tech Company News

**[Rubi Laboratories]{.underline}**

On a mission to reimagine supply chains, Rubi Laboratories (NSF-2414329)
develops technology that captures carbon dioxide and converts it into
cellulose, which is then spun into fibers and textiles. The company is
partnering with Walmart. Article via The Cool Down

**[Tandem PV]{.underline}**

To capture a broader spectrum of sunlight and convert more energy into
electricity, Tandem PV (NSF-1747302) develops ultra-thin and efficient
perovskite-based solar panels. The company recently raised \$50 million.
Article via Inc.

**[CelluDot]{.underline}**

To improve crop protection and boost agricultural productivity, CelluDot
(NSF-2304528) develops a plant-based formula that reduces chemical and
pesticide drift. CelluDot is located in the Arkansas Research and
Technology Park, which has generated \$346 million in economic impact.
Article via University of Arkansas

**[Diligent Robotics]{.underline}**

Diligent Robotics (NSF-1738375), creator of the "Moxi" robot assistant,
helps healthcare workers with non-patient facing tasks like delivering
supplies and medications. The company reports that Moxi made over 1
million deliveries in hospitals across the country. Article via The
Robot Report

**[Kilele Health]{.underline}**

Kilele Health (NSF-2335105) helps people with cardiometabolic diseases
by allowing doctors and patients to remotely monitor health using
SunVida, a wearable device designed for chronic disease management.
Article via University of Cincinnati

**[MedMicroMaps]{.underline}**

MedMicroMaps (NSF-2432936) develops an immersive learning platform to
help users learn microbiology and infectious diseases. Nursing students
at Montana State University Billings tried out the company's virtual
reality prototype that allows students to see and interact with
microbes, bacteria and viruses to treat a patient with a sore throat.
Article via KTVQ

**[Next Energy Technologies]{.underline}**

Next Energy Technologies (NSF-1353618) develops window coating that
generates electricity. The company upgraded its pilot production line to
include 40-by 60-inch laminated transparent power-generating windows.
Article via USGlass magazine

**[PSYONIC]{.underline}**

To improve the lives of people with amputations and injured limbs,
PSYONIC (NSF-1951382) develops durable touch-sensing bionic hands. In
2024, the company appeared on ABC's "Shark Tank" television show and was
able to increase revenue and build its team as a result. Article via
SlashGear

**[SafelyYou]{.underline}**

SafelyYou (NSF-1758539) develops artificial intelligence to monitor
falls for patients with Alzheimer's and other forms of dementia. The
company recently raised \$43 million. Article via Pulse 2.0

**[SixLine Semiconductor]{.underline}**

SixLine Semiconductor (NSF-2322200) develops carbon nanomaterials that
can move electricity faster than traditional semiconductors made with
silicon. The company's CEO and co-founder was selected for the Forbes 30
Under 30 List for 2025 in manufacturing and industry. Article via
University of Wisconsin-Madison

**[Solestial]{.underline}**

Solestial (NSF-2052381) develops solar panels for space using silicon
photovoltaic cells. The company was featured in Google's 2025 Super Bowl
ad campaign spotlighting 50 businesses from 50 states using artificial
intelligence. Article via KJZZ

**[Symbrosia]{.underline}**

Symbrosia (NSF-2309372) develops seaweed-based supplements to reduce
methane emissions from livestock. The red seaweed called "SeaGraze"
inhibits the formation of methane in a cow's digestion system without
impacting the cow's nutrition or milk production. Article via
AgFunderNews

**[UbiQD]{.underline}**

Using quantum dot technology, UbiQD (NSF-1758697) converts sunlight into
electricity for use in agriculture, energy and security. UbiQD recently
acquired BlueDot Photonics, a developer of solar panels and photonic
devices. Article via Los Alamos Reporter

**[Urban Sky]{.underline}**

Developer of the "Microballoon," Urban Sky (NSF-2112381) designs and
operates stratospheric balloon systems for remote sensing applications
for commercial use, such as wildfire monitoring and disaster response.
The company recently raised \$30 million. Article via SpaceNews

**[Cyclopure]{.underline}**

To keep drinking water safe, Cyclopure (NSF-1831206) develops testing
kits and reusable plant-based filters to test for and remove
contaminants and per- and polyfluoroalkyl substances (PFAS). Article via
Portland Press Herald

**[Filament Games]{.underline}**

To help people gain new skills and knowledge, Filament Games
(NSF-1853206) develops engaging and accessible educational games for all
ages. The company develops iCivics,a platform and series of games to
help educators, as well as FIRMM-pix that helps patients stay calm
during brain magnetic resonance imaging. Article via Madison Magazine

**[Microbial Insights]{.underline}**

Microbial Insights (NSF-1056963) uses molecular biological tools to
identify and quantify microorganisms for environmental remediation,
water and agriculture. The company is working to identify new biomarkers
for predicting and preventing corrosion, partnering to clean up a
polluted lagoon in Ghana, and working alongside a team of scientists to
identify and overcome challenges for upcoming deep space exploration.
Article via Knox News

**[Natrx]{.underline}**

To promote biodiversity and coastal restoration, Natrx (NSF-2322073)
provides nature-based coastal protection alternatives. The company
develops artificial reefs called "ExoForms," an ecofriendly barrier that
provides shoreline protection and promotes habitat growth. Natrx
collaborated with an East Carolina University researcher to find a more
sustainable process to produce concrete. Article via ECU News Services

**[NeuroFlow]{.underline}**

Veteran-founded NeuroFlow (NSF-1951062) provides a digital mental health
platform that partners with major healthcare providers to drive better
health outcomes and lower care costs. The company recently acquired a
health analytics model to power more artificial intelligence innovation.
Article via MSN

**[Nth Cycle]{.underline}**

Metal refining company Nth Cycle (NSF-2112301) develops a process to
repurpose metals found in batteries for electronic vehicles and consumer
technology. The company is currently building several modular refining
facilities to recycle end-of-life metals. Article via Waste360

**[Quantifind]{.underline}**

Quantifind (NSF-1026493) uses artificial intelligence (AI) and data
science for anti-money laundering and fraud detection. The company's AI
platform helps identify malicious activity quickly and accurately in the
financial services and public sector domains. Article via FinTech Global

**[Silvis Materials]{.underline}**

To replace petroleum materials used in coatings, adhesives and paints,
Silvis Materials (NSF-2415714) develops building materials made from
plants and trees. The company announced a partnership with Oak Ridge
National Laboratory to develop bio-based adhesives without harmful
chemicals like formaldehyde. Article via BioSourced

**[Upstream Biotechnology]{.underline}**

Upstream Biotechnology (NSF-2132421) develops plants that respond to
pathogen-specific stimuli by releasing immune proteins to combat deadly
pathogens. The company is partnering with Bene Seeds, a tomato company,
to create new tomato varieties with stronger resistance to disease.
Article via The Packer

**[Yank Technologies]{.underline}**

Yank Technologies (NSF-2406644) develops powerful long-distance wireless
charging technology for automative, consumer, space exploration and
consumer needs. Forbes recently listed the company as a mobility startup
shaping 2025.

### Internal\-\--Disease\-\--2NC

#### **The NSF is key to [rapid]{.underline}, effective [pandemic]{.underline} response.**

**Tabarrok '23** \[Maxwell Tabarrok is a pre-doctoral researcher at
Dartmouth College, working with Heidi Williams on the economics of
science, 7-17-2023, \"How the NSF Moved Faster than the NIH During
COVID-19\", Institute for Progress,
https://ifp.org/how-the-nsf-moved-faster-than-the-nih/\]//LASA-AR

[COVID-19 tested **every aspect**]{.underline} of the scientific
apparatus, but it [put **unique strains** on the systems that allocate
funding]{.underline}. Some funding mechanisms managed to allocate
resources with dispatch. Operation Warp Speed (OWS), the most obvious
example, showed that [smart federal funding could generate **incredible
breakthroughs** for society at a remarkable pace.]{.underline} At the
same time, many features of the federal response proved too slow,
failing to prevent avoidable sickness and death.

[It was **incredibly important** to maximize the speed at which pandemic
research was]{.underline} supported and [rolled out, and multiple
federal agencies tried to **quickly deploy** resources to
pandemic-related science.]{.underline} But [despite a]{.underline}
near-[universal sense of motivation]{.underline} to move quickly, [the
country's largest scientific]{.underline} [funding agencies delivered
**disparate outcomes**]{.underline}. [The **N**ational **S**cience
**F**oundation]{.underline} (NSF) managed to [ramp up COVID-19-related
granting four months faster than the National Institutes of
Health]{.underline} (NIH); a remarkable pace during an emergency when
every day mattered. The results suggest that policymakers should grant
additional flexibility for our science funding institutions to expedite
review during crises.

Comparing the NIH and NSF

[The basic story of]{.underline} scientific [grant funding]{.underline}
during the pandemic [is that while the NIH spent **more money**, the NSF
was able to act]{.underline} much [fast]{.underline}er in getting grants
out the door. The following graph compares the monthly granting rates of
the NIH (purple) and NSF (orange). Grants are marked as pandemic-related
if they contain words like "pandemic," "Covid," "SARS-CoV-2," or other
related terms in their title or description.

The [**difference in speed** for issuing pandemic-related
grants]{.underline} immediately jumps out in March of 2020. The NSF's
[pandemic granting overtook the NIH]{.underline}, and remained ahead for
the next 5 months. The NSF only put out its call for pandemic-related
grants in April 2020, yet it hit the peak of its granting the following
month. The NIH's granting didn't peak until September 2020, even though
it had been accepting proposals since February 2020. As we'll see, its
most successful rapid grants program mirrored some of the strengths of
the NSF.

To be clear, in dollar-weighted terms, the NIH would spend about 10
times more than the NSF on pandemic-related projects over 2020. But this
shouldn't be surprising, given that the NIH has five times the budget of
the NSF and received 13 times more pandemic research stimulus money in
April of 2020. What is surprising is that, [when the world needed it
most, the NSF was able to **bootstrap** a funding program in months, in
a field outside its usual purview,]{.underline} rivaling an organization
five times its size.

The [NSF's pandemic spending responded to]{.underline} the [**increased
importance** of timing]{.underline}. While in normal years, both
organizations have disbursed only about half the year's money by May,
the NSF spiked its pandemic spending in May 2020. While the NIH did
amplify its funding of pandemic-related research, it stayed roughly
within the normal schedule of the organization: a slow ramp to a peak in
September, and a complete pause in October. The NIH didn't schedule its
funding of pandemic research in 2020 all that differently than in 2019.
In normal years, the returns to science funding don't vary much from
month-to-month. But during a crisis like COVID-19, a one-month
difference in the vaccine research timeline could have saved tens of
thousands of lives.

Despite its encompassing name, the National Science Foundation was seen
as the "puny partner" of federal science funding when it was created,
and it still wields a smaller budget and less influence than similar
agencies today. But [the NSF went from funding **essentially zero**
pandemic-related research in the past decade to sending **over 800**
pandemic-related grants out the door, with \$140 million dollars
attached in a **single year.**]{.underline} How did the historically
puny partner make such a big turnaround into a field it had
comparatively little funding expertise in?

### Internal\-\--BioFoundries\-\--2NC

#### NSF BioFoundries are the [lynchpin]{.underline} of cutting edge scientific research. They [enable]{.underline} research at [scale]{.underline}.

**NSF '23** \[National Science Foundation; May 10; Independent agency of
the United States federal government that supports fundamental research
and education in all the non-medical fields of science and engineering,
funds approximately 25% of all federally supported basic research
conducted by the United States\' colleges and universities; National
Science Foundation, "NSF 23-585: BioFoundries to Enable Access to
Infrastructure and Resources for Advancing Modern Biology and
Biotechnology (BioFoundries),"
nsf-gov-resources.nsf.gov/solicitations/pubs/2023/nsf23585/nsf23585.pdf?VersionId=a6jeVs1JATKFc3wpTO2HAAcv5dD7q.38\]
//jwh

Grand challenge [questions **requir**]{.underline}ing [a **deeper**
understanding of biological systems]{.underline} and technologies are as
diverse as life itself. [Understanding]{.underline} the complexity of
[**living** systems and their **interaction**]{.underline} with
human-derived products and processes, **[ensur]{.underline}**ing
**[safe]{.underline}**, [**ethical** and **equitable**]{.underline}
access [to]{.underline} and [**co-generation** of]{.underline} knowledge
and products, requires the [sustained **development** of
technologies]{.underline}, sophisticated instrumentation, workflow
pipelines and their automation, and advanced computing that are beyond
the capabilities found in the laboratories of individual investigators.
[**Broad access** to these tools]{.underline}, workflows, processes, and
knowledge bases in a facility that [is capable of **bespoke**
design]{.underline} and process scale-up, in response to user needs, is
essential [for addressing **grand** challenges and]{.underline}
translating the **[knowledge]{.underline}** created into applications
for the bioeconomy, to meet societal and national needs.

Recognizing this gap, [**NSF** is establishing the **BioFoundries**
Program]{.underline}. NSF envisions its BioFoundries
[serving]{.underline} both [as **user** facilities and as **innovation**
hubs for cutting-edge]{.underline}, multidisciplinary [research
with]{.underline} the important role of [democratizing access
to]{.underline} the tools of [**modern** biology]{.underline} and
biotechnology [for]{.underline} the purposes of [**advancing**
scientific discoveries]{.underline} and translating those discoveries to
address pressing societal challenges. [A BioFoundry is an **integrated**
facility that combines **biological**]{.underline}, **[chemical
biology]{.underline}**, [and engineering biology systems]{.underline}
and tools [with automation]{.underline}, high throughput measurement,
integrated data acquisition and analysis, [and a]{.underline}rtificial
[i]{.underline}ntelligence (AI) and machine learning [to enable
**feedback loops** that facilitate progressive]{.underline} end-to-end
[cycles of **design**]{.underline}, **[build]{.underline}**,
**[test]{.underline}**, [and **learn**]{.underline}. In addition to
providing broad access, [a **BioFoundry** develops
innovative]{.underline} (bio)**[tech]{.underline}**nologies, [invents
**new** processes and workflows]{.underline}, integrates advanced
computation, [and creates]{.underline} reproducible [results and
**shareable** knowledge]{.underline}-bases [to **accelerate**
the]{.underline} design-build-test-learn [pipeline from **basic**
research to translation]{.underline}. By applying the same streamlining
and automation principles that continue [to revolutionize
manufacturing]{.underline}, **[BioFoundries]{.underline}** can [operate
with **speed** and at **scales** not possible in **individual**
laboratories or]{.underline} typical [university]{.underline} core
[facilities]{.underline}.

The NSF BioFoundries Program will [address a **critical** need for
national infrastructure to **support** biotech]{.underline}nology and
the bioeconomy, which has been highlighted by recent publications1, 2,
workshop reports3, 4, 5, white papers6, 7, the U.S. White House
Executive Order on Advancing Biotechnology and Biomanufacturing
Innovation for a Sustainable, Safe, and Secure American Bioeconomy8, and
the ensuing reports on Bold Goals for U.S. Biotechnology and
Biomanufacturing9. [The **BioFoundries** Program]{.underline} also
aligns with, and [follows]{.underline} naturally [from]{.underline},
[NSF\'s **investments** in]{.underline} diverse areas of biological
[sciences]{.underline}, chemical biology, biotechnology and
bioengineering through programs such as: Enabling Discovery through
Genomic Tools; NeuroNex; Plant Genome Research Program; Dimensions of
Biodiversity (now Biodiversity on a Changing Planet); Organismal
Response to Climate Change; Future Manufacturing; Cyber-Physical
Systems; Smart Health and Biomedical Research in the Era of Artificial
Intelligence; Foundations of Emerging Technologies; Molecular
Foundations of Biotechnology; Reproducible Cells and Organoids via
Directed Differentiation Encoding; [**Accelerating** Innovations in
Biomanufacturing]{.underline} Approaches [through
**Collaboration**]{.underline} Between NSF and the DOE BETO funding
Agile Biofoundry and others. The foundational or use-inspired scientific
research drivers for NSF BioFoundries could leverage these prior
investments or address any other field of science and engineering
supported by NSF.

[BioFoundries]{.underline} are intended to [empower the **research**
community by providing the **infrastructure**]{.underline} needed [to
match **new tech**]{.underline}nologies [to]{.underline} specific
research drivers and [**facilitate** transformative discoveries through
**cutting-edge** research]{.underline} as well as broadening access,
training, de facto standardization, centralization, and integration of
data and technologies. [By providing this **infrastructure** at
**scale**]{.underline} with broad opportunity for access, [each
BioFoundry would also offer **research** and **training** opportunities
for a **diversity** of institutions]{.underline}, including R1 and
non-R1 research universities, primarily undergraduate and
minority-serving institutions, and institutions in EPSCoR jurisdictions,
and increase the capacity of a scientific workforce prepared to meet the
challenges of the 21st century and improve the bioeconomy. In short, NSF
envisions BioFoundries embodying Sydney Brenner\'s famous assertion that
\"Progress in science depends on new techniques, new discoveries, and
new ideas, probably in that order.\"

#### NSF BioFoundries [streamline]{.underline} tech to [advance]{.underline} the bioeconomy. That solves \[x\].

**NSF '24** \[National Science Foundation; August 28; Independent agency
of the United States federal government that supports fundamental
research and education in all the non-medical fields of science and
engineering, funds approximately 25% of all federally supported basic
research conducted by the United States\' colleges and universities;
National Science Foundation, "NSF invests in BioFoundries to drive
advances across science and engineering,"
nsf.gov/news/nsf-invests-biofoundries-drive-advances-across-science\]
//jwh

[NSF BioFoundries]{.underline} are integrated facilities that
[**enable** researchers to rapidly **design**]{.underline},
**[create]{.underline}**, [**test** and **streamline**]{.underline} [the
development of]{.underline} tools and [products to **accelerate**
research to **advance** the bioeconomy]{.underline}.

These facilities [combine the **latest** advances in **biological**
sciences]{.underline}, **[geosciences]{.underline}**,
**[biomaterials]{.underline}**, **[chemical biology]{.underline}** [and
**bioengineering** with]{.underline} tool development,
**[automation]{.underline}**, high throughput measurement, [integrated
data]{.underline} acquisition and [analysis]{.underline}, [and
**a**]{.underline}rtificial **[i]{.underline}**ntelligence [and
**m**]{.underline}achine **[l]{.underline}**earning. By serving as
user-facing facilities with complementary internal research programs,
NSF BioFoundries will [provide **broad** access to **cutting-edge
tech**]{.underline}nology, **[workflows]{.underline}**, [**processes**
and **knowledge** bases]{.underline}, and the design and scale-up
capabilities necessary to ensure all ideas reach their potential.

[Each **NSF BioFoundry**]{.underline} will [focus on a **different**
area of biology]{.underline} or biotechnology, [but]{.underline} all
will [**advance** both in-house and user-initiated
projects]{.underline}, [**train** the **next gen**]{.underline}eration
[of the scientific **workforce**]{.underline}, engage with consumers and
users of the products developed, [and]{.underline} continually [enhance
workflows]{.underline} and processes [to **accelerate** the
**translation** of ideas]{.underline}.

\"Across all fields of science and engineering, [addressing **grand**
challenges requires access to **advanced tech**]{.underline}nologies,
sophisticated instrumentation and workflows, [but not every
**researcher**]{.underline} at every institution [can access
those]{.underline} critical [capabilities]{.underline},\" said NSF
Director Sethuraman Panchanathan. \"[The **new NSF**
**BioFoundries**]{.underline} will help [**democratize** access to
**critical** research infrastructure]{.underline}, helping to [**spur**
opportunities everywhere so **innovation** can happen
**anywhere**]{.underline}. Not only will these NSF BioFoundries advance
biology, they also will lead to developments in artificial intelligence,
data storage, health, climate resilience and more.\"

The facilities will drastically expand and democratize biotechnology
capabilities in the United States. To date, [such
**tech**]{.underline}nologies have [been **limited** to **specific**
academic labs]{.underline}, certain parts of industry and two government
facilities: the U.S. Department of Energy\'s Agile BioFoundry and the
National Institute of Standards and Technology\'s Living Measurement
Systems Foundry. [By acting as **user** facilities]{.underline} without
charging user fees, [NSF BioFoundries]{.underline} will [enable
**research** and **translation** at **various**
institutions]{.underline} --- from research-intensive universities to
minority-serving institutions, community colleges and others.

Education, training and outreach efforts will [**further** the
facilities\' reach]{.underline}, [**engaging** students]{.underline}
from kindergarten through graduate school, [supporting **industry** and
**translational** partnerships]{.underline}, [and creating **materials**
to **educate** the **public** on **engineering** biology]{.underline},
[**synthetic** biology and **biotech**]{.underline}nology. Several NSF
BioFoundries will work with minority-serving institutions, including
historically Black colleges and universities, to bring in groups
traditionally underrepresented in the STEM workforce. Efforts will focus
on training a workforce skilled in biology, computer science, AI and
robotics.

#### NSF BioFoundries ensure the [transmission]{.underline} of data, which is key to data [processing]{.underline} and [standardization]{.underline}.

**NSF '23** \[National Science Foundation; May 10; Independent agency of
the United States federal government that supports fundamental research
and education in all the non-medical fields of science and engineering,
funds approximately 25% of all federally supported basic research
conducted by the United States\' colleges and universities; National
Science Foundation, "NSF 23-585: BioFoundries to Enable Access to
Infrastructure and Resources for Advancing Modern Biology and
Biotechnology (BioFoundries),"
nsf-gov-resources.nsf.gov/solicitations/pubs/2023/nsf23585/nsf23585.pdf?VersionId=a6jeVs1JATKFc3wpTO2HAAcv5dD7q.38\]
//jwh

The goal of this solicitation is to support the establishment of
[BioFoundries]{.underline} that [focus on]{.underline} the
**[innovation]{.underline}** pipeline [and **research**]{.underline},
while [ensuring **broad** access to]{.underline} the research
**[resources]{.underline}**, **[training]{.underline}**,
**[education]{.underline}**, [and **outreach** to catalyze]{.underline}
our understanding of biological or [**bio-engineered**

[for **rapid** translation of]{.underline} basic [**discoveries** into

[process]{.underline}. The user-facing activities of the BioFoundry must
[ensure]{.underline} that [these advances are **accessible** to the

**[data]{.underline}**, [and **scientific** practices]{.underline} that
will make possible the integration of data from multiple
sources/databases and allow the construction of data analysis pipelines
using heterogeneous software data analysis and AI tools that communicate

[BioFoundries]{.underline} are expected to [include

both **[human]{.underline}**- [and **machineusable**

BioFoundries are envisioned to combine characteristics of user
facilities and research centers in a scientific ecosystem that includes:

**[resources]{.underline}**, **[samples]{.underline}**,

### Internal\-\--Global Science\-\--AT: Other Institutions\-\--NSF Key\-\--2NC

#### The NSF is [key]{.underline} to leveraging the [bioeconomy]{.underline}.

**NSF '24** \[National Science Foundation; October 2; Independent agency
of the United States federal government that supports fundamental
research and education in all the non-medical fields of science and
engineering, funds approximately 25% of all federally supported basic
research conducted by the United States\' colleges and universities;
National Science Foundation, "NSF and international partners to invest
\$82M in 6 Global Centers in 2024,"
nsf.gov/news/nsf-international-partners-invest-82m-six-2024-global\]
//jwh

Today, [the]{.underline} U.S. [National Science
Foundation]{.underline}and partner agencies in the U.S., Canada,
Finland, Japan, the Republic of Korea (ROK), and the United Kingdom
[announced **funding**]{.underline} awards [totaling]{.underline} nearly
[**\$82 million** in their Global Centers **competition**]{.underline}.

The 2024 Global Centers awards [focus on **advancing bioeconomy**
research to solve global challenges]{.underline}, [whether]{.underline}
by increasing [**crop** resilience]{.underline}, converting [**plant**
matter]{.underline} or other biomass [into fuel]{.underline}, or
[**paving** the way for biofoundries]{.underline} to scale up
applications of biotechnology for societal benefit. The program
[supports **holistic**]{.underline}, [**multidisciplinary** projects
that bring together **international** teams and **scientific
disciplines**]{.underline}, including education and social sciences,
necessary to achieve use-inspired outcomes. All Global Centers will
integrate public engagement and workforce development, paying close
attention to impacts on communities.

\"The Global Centers are [**leveraging expertise** and **resources**
across like-minded nations]{.underline} and uniting multidisciplinary

Partner agencies include the National Endowment for the Humanities in

the ROK Ministry of Science and ICT and the National Research Foundation

Biological Sciences Research Council.

\"Alongside replacing fossil fuels, there is an urgent need to replace
petrochemical industrial feedstocks across a wide range of sectors. This
is a global challenge that requires global solutions, and UKRI is
delighted to be partnering in the NSF Global Centers 2024 programme to
meet this need,\" said UKRI CEO Dame Ottoline Leyser. \"The announcement
today will be at the forefront of real-world solutions, from improved
recycling to new bioplastics, building a sustainable circular economy.
The centers will create the global networks and skills needed to drive a
thriving bioeconomy benefitting all.\"

\"The joint bioeconomy funding call is a response to global challenges
resolvable only with international scientific cooperation. The Research
Council of Finland is proud to be part of this highly impactful
multilateral research funding cooperation for the best of our planet and
its citizens,\" said RCF President Paula Eerola.

\"Business Finland has high expectations for NSF Global Centers
Bioeconomy Program\'s use-inspired approach, where the best Finnish
bioeconomy companies and researchers get to work closely with best

solutions,\" says Lassi Noponen, director general of Business Finland.

Yoo Sang-im, Korea\'s Minister of Science and ICT, touted South Korea\'s
participation in the NSF Global Centers program for the first time,
\"The Korean government is expanding its support for global research to
foster continued collaboration and connections. We look forward to
seeing the joint efforts of biofoundry and bioeconomy produce remarkable
results over the next five years.\"

Calling the Global Centers program \"a groundbreaking initiative,\" JST
President Hashimoto Kazuhito noted how Japan would help to \"strengthen
global bioeconomy research capabilities and develop talent through
endeavors such as the establishment of biofoundries ... We eagerly look
forward to the future breakthroughs these efforts will bring.\"

\"[Today\'s **global** challenges demand **unprecedented**
**collaboration** and **innovation**]{.underline}. [The N]{.underline}SF
Global Centers initiative [is **transforming** how we address these
**challenges** by supporting **international**]{.underline},
